Saints like Tulsidas, Surdas, and Mirabai were prominent Saguna bhaktas.\n- **Nirguna Bhakti:** This stream involves the worship of a formless, attributeless, and abstract God (nirguna). It emphasizes knowledge (jnana) and inner realization over idol worship and rituals. Saints like Kabir and Guru Nanak were leading figures of the Nirguna tradition.\n\n **   **\n\n-              :\n- ** :**         ()                 ,  , , ,   ,         \n- ** :**     ,     ()                              ",

Both fall under the umbrella of Saguna Bhakti.\n\n **    **\n\n- **  :**         ,            \n- **  :**            ,           ,       \n- **  :**         ,                   ",

    "question": "The moment of inertia of a rigid body about an axis:",
    "question_hi": "         ",
    "options": [
      "Is a scalar quantity, independent of the axis",
      "Is a vector quantity, dependent on the axis",
      "Is a scalar quantity, dependent on the axis",
      "Is a tensor quantity, independent of the axis"
    ],
    "options_hi": [
      "   ,     ",
      "   ,     ",
      "   ,     ",
      "   ,     "
    ],
    "correct": "Is a scalar quantity, dependent on the axis",
    "explanation": {
      "summary": "Correct Answer: C) Is a scalar quantity, dependent on the axis\n\n : C)    ,     ",
      "analysis_correct": " **Why this is correct**\n\n- **Scalar Quantity:** Moment of inertia is a measure of an object's resistance to rotational acceleration. It is defined by the sum of the product of the mass of each particle and the square of its distance from the axis of rotation (I = mr). Since mass and distance squared are both scalars, their product and sum are also scalar. It has magnitude but no direction.\n- **Dependent on the axis:** The value of the moment of inertia critically depends on the choice of the axis of rotation. The distribution of mass is measured *relative* to this axis. For the same body, the moment of inertia will be different for different axes.\n\n **   **\n\n- ** :**                               (I = mr)              ,                 \n- **  :**                       **        , -      - ",
      "analysis_incorrect": " **Why other options are incorrect**\n\n- It is not a vector quantity because it does not have a direction associated with it.\n- It is not independent of the axis; changing the axis of rotation changes how the mass is distributed relative to it, thus changing the moment of inertia (as per the Parallel Axis Theorem and Perpendicular Axis Theorem).\n- While technically a component of the inertia tensor, in introductory physics and for a fixed axis, it is treated as a scalar, and it is not independent of the axis.\n\n **    **\n\n-              \n-      ;                  ,        (        )\n-          ,         ,         ,       ",
      "conclusion": " **Concluding Note**\n\n- Understanding that moment of inertia is a scalar that depends on the axis is fundamental to solving problems in rotational dynamics. It is the rotational analog of mass in linear motion.\n\n ****\n\n-               ,                    ",
      "fact": " **Interesting Fact**\n\n- An object has its minimum moment of inertia when the axis of rotation passes through its center of mass. Any other parallel axis will result in a larger moment of inertia.\n\n ** **\n\n-                                "
    }

      "conclusion": " **Concluding Note**\n\n- Orbiting is essentially a state of continuous freefall.
